\section{What you really need to know about DataSet and TwoSet}

All models use training and test sets.  The model builds itself on the
training set, and is tested on the test set.  {\em DataSet} is
designed for generating and manipulating training and test sets, and
{\em TwoSet} is a special object that is designed to calculate
accuracies for one-output models {\em only}.

All training and test sets are expressed by Matrices in the following
format, where $i$ is an input to the model, and $o$ is an output:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{cccccccc}
$i_{11}$ & $i_{12}$ & $\ldots$ & $i_{1I}$ & $o_{11}$ & $o_{12}$ &
$\ldots$ & $o_{1O}$ \\
$i_{21}$ & $i_{22}$ & $\ldots$ & $i_{2I}$ & $o_{21}$ & $o_{22}$ &
$\ldots$ & $o_{2O}$ \\
$i_{31}$ & $i_{32}$ & $\ldots$ & $i_{3I}$ & $o_{31}$ & $o_{32}$ &
$\ldots$ & $o_{3O}$ \\
$\ldots$ & $\ldots$ & $\ldots$ & $\ldots$ & $\ldots$ & $\ldots$ &
$\ldots$ & $\ldots$ \\
$i_{N1}$ & $i_{N2}$ & $\ldots$ & $i_{NI}$ & $o_{N1}$ & $o_{N2}$ &
$\ldots$ & $o_{NO}$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

for $N$ examples (``exemplars'') in the set, $I$ inputs and $O$
outputs.  Although there are many highly useful features in {\em
DataSet}, a programmer building a {\em Model} need only know how to
access the training and test Matrices, whether they've been loaded
into the {\em DataSet} object, how many inputs and outputs there are,
whether the output is discrete (held to 0 or 1), and the number of
examples in each set.  These methods are shown in \beginred red
\endcolor in the {\em DataSet} specification.

In addition, the programmer of a {\em Model} needs only to understand
that the methods setTrainTwoSet and setTestTwoSet must be called in
order for the method metricsReport, which reports accuracy metrics of
a one-output {\em Model}, to be called.  These methods are also shown
in \beginred red \endcolor in the {\em DataSet} specification.

\section{DataSet}

{\em DataSet} is a standalone object that contains and manipulates the
dataset that {\em Model} will access.  All {\em DataSets} have raw,
training and test datasets, and know the number of inputs and
outputs. {\em DataSet} consists of:

\begin{itemize}

\item {\em Public} methods

\begin{itemize}

\item Raw dataset methods:
\begin{itemize}
\item void loadRaw( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and loads a file into the $Raw$ dataset
      Matrix, returns nothing
\item bool rawLoaded(): Returns a flag to indicate if $Raw$ dataset is
      loaded
\item Matrix$<$ double $>$\& getRawMatrix(): returns the $Raw$ dataset
\item void setRawMatrix( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $inMatrix$ ): loads
      Matrix $inMatrix$ into the $Raw$ dataset Matrix
\end{itemize}

\item Training set methods:
\begin{itemize}
\item void loadTrain( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and loads a file into the $TrainSetData$
      Matrix, returns nothing
\item \beginred bool trainLoaded(): Returns a flag to indicate if
      $TrainSetData$ Matrix is loaded \endcolor
\item bool saveTrain( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and saves the $TrainSetData$ Matrix into a
      file, returns flag indicating if save operation was successful
\item bool saveScales( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and saves the scaling factors for the
      training set into the file, returns flag indicating if save
      operation was successful

{\bf Note:} see \hyperref[normEqn]{equation \ref{normEqn}} below for
an explanation of scaling.  Saved to the file will be $lbound$ (1
value), and for each $x$, $S$ and $min$.
\begin{equation}
x_{norm} = lbound + S(x - min)
\end{equation}
where $x_{norm}$ is the normalized result, $lbound$ is the lower limit
of normalized values, $S$ is the scaling factor, and $min$ is the
minimum value of each $x$ in the dataset.

\item \beginred Matrix$<$ double $>$\& getTrainMatrix(): returns the
      $TrainSetData$ Matrix \endcolor
\item void setTrainMatrix( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $inMatrix$ ): loads
      Matrix $inMatrix$ into the $TrainSetData$ Matrix
\end{itemize}

\item {\em TwoSet} accessors for the training set:
\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred bool setTrainTwoSet(): Constructs an internal {\em
      TwoSet} object from the training set, populating the `real'
      column of the {\em TwoSet} object with the output column from
      the training set {\em Matrix}, returns true if successful
      \endcolor
\item TwoSet\& getTrainTwoSet(): Returns the constructed {\em TwoSet}
      object from the training set
\item bool TrainTwoSetLoaded(): Returns true if the internal training
      {\em TwoSet} object was successfully constructed
\item bool saveTrainTwoSet( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and saves the {\em TwoSet} object from the
      training set into a file, returns flag indicating if save
      operation was successful
\end{itemize}

\item Test set methods:
\begin{itemize}
\item void loadTest( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and loads a file into the $TestSetData$
      Matrix, returns nothing
\item \beginred bool testLoaded(): Returns a flag to indicate if
      $TestSetData$ Matrix is loaded \endcolor
\item bool saveTest( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and saves the $TestSetData$ Matrix into a
      file, returns flag indicating if save operation was successful
\item \beginred Matrix$<$ double $>$\& getTestMatrix(): returns the
      $TestSetData$ Matrix \endcolor
\item void setTestMatrix( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $inMatrix$ ): loads
      Matrix $inMatrix$ into the $TestSetData$ Matrix
\end{itemize}

\item {\em TwoSet} accessors for the test set:
\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred bool setTestTwoSet(): Constructs an internal {\em
      TwoSet} object from the test set, populating the `real' column
      of the {\em TwoSet} object with the output column from the test
      set {\em Matrix}, returns true if successful \endcolor
\item TwoSet\& getTestTwoSet(): Returns the constructed {\em TwoSet}
      object from the test set
\item bool TestTwoSetLoaded(): Returns true if the internal test {\em
      TwoSet} object was successfully constructed
\item bool saveTestTwoSet( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as
      argument (string), and saves the {\em TwoSet} object from the
      test set into a file, returns flag indicating if save operation
      was successful
\end{itemize}

\item General methods:
\begin{itemize}
\item void setInput( unsigned $I$ ): Set the number of input nodes to
      $I$
\item \beginred unsigned getInput(): Returns number of input nodes
      \endcolor
\item void setOutput( unsigned $O$ ): Set the number of output nodes
      to $O$
\item \beginred unsigned getOutput(): Returns number of output nodes
      \endcolor
\item \beginred unsigned getNumTrain(): Returns the number of
      examples in $TrainSetData$, the training set \endcolor
\item \beginred unsigned getNumTest(): Returns the number of examples
      in $TestSetData$, the test set \endcolor
\item \beginred void removeInputs( vector$<$ unsigned $>$\& $v$ ):
      Given a vector representing input positions within a dataset,
      removes the corresponding columns in $Raw$, $TrainSetData$, and
      $TestSetData$, thus effectively removing those inputs from a
      dataset \endcolor
\end{itemize}

{\bf Note:} In the following methods, a variate $x$ is normalized
according to:
\begin{equation}\label{normEqn}
x_{norm} = lbound + [({{x - min} \over {max - min}}) \times (hbound -
lbound)]
\end{equation}
where $x_{norm}$ is the normalized value, $lbound$ is the lower limit
of the normalized result, $hbound$ is the upper limit of the
normalized result, $min$ is the minimum value of $x$ in the dataset,
and $max$ is the maximum value of $x$ in the dataset.

\item Methods related to normalizing dataset variates
\begin{itemize}
\item void setInUpper( double $x$ ): Sets the upper limit of
      normalized input variates to $x$
\item double getInUpper(): Returns the upper limit of normalized input
      variates
\item void setInLower( double $x$ ): Sets the lower limit of
      normalized input variates to $x$
\item double getInLower(): Returns the lower limit of normalized input
      variates
\item void setOutUpper( double $x$ ): Sets the upper limit of
      normalized output variates to $x$
\item double getOutUpper(): Returns the upper limit of normalized
      output variates
\item void setOutLower( double $x$ ): Sets the lower limit of
      normalized output variates to $x$
\item double getOutLower(): Returns the lower limit of normalized
      output variates
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to identifying output as discrete (held to 0 or
      1)
\begin{itemize}
\item void setDiscrete( bool $flag$ ): Sets output to be discrete if
      $flag$ is true, continuous if false
\item \beginred bool getDiscrete(): Returns the state of discrete
      output \endcolor
\item void setThreshold( double $x$ ): Sets the threshold value for
      discrete output to be $x$, any output $>= x$ will be 1, and any
      output $< x$ will be 0
\item double getThreshold(): Returns the threshold value
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to transforming datasets:
\begin{itemize}
\item bool raw2train(): Converts the raw dataset to a training set by
      normalizing its input variates, and if nondiscrete, its output
      variates, returning true if the operation was successful
\item bool randomize( unsigned $n$ ): Randomizes the raw dataset into
      training and test sets which are then normalized, preserving the
      frequency of outcomes in the training and test sets, takes the
      number of exemplars $n$ to be placed in the test set as an
      argument {\em ( number of outputs must be only 1, and it must be
      discrete )}, returns true if successful
\item bool randomize( unsigned $n$, unsigned $d$ ): Randomizes the raw
      dataset into training and test sets which are then normalized,
      preserving the frequency of outcomes in the training and test
      sets, takes the numerator $n$ and denominator $d$ of a fraction
      representing the portion of examplars to be placed in the test
      set as arguments, {\em ( number of outputs must be only 1, and
      it must be discrete )}, returns true if successful
\item bool randomizeD( double $x$ ): Randomizes the raw dataset into
      training and test sets which are then normalized, preserving the
      frequency of outcomes in the training and test sets, takes the
      fraction $x$ as a double value from 0 to 1 representing the
      portion of examplars to be placed in the test set as argument
      {\em ( number of outputs must be only 1, and it must be discrete
      )}, returns true if successful
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to calculating accuracy
\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred void metricsReport( ostream\& $streamObj$ ): Outputs
      all internal {\em TwoSet} metrics for a 1-output {\em Model},
      takes ostream object $streamObj$ as argument \endcolor
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to logging to the history file:
\begin{itemize}
\item void setHistory( bool $flag$ ): Sets history logging, which {\em
      appends} all operations to the history file
\item bool getHistory(): Returns the current state of history logging
\item void setHistoryFilename( string\& $filename$ ): Sets the name of
      the history file to string $filename$
\item bool addHistory( ostringstream\& $outputStream$ ): appends the
      ostringstream object $outputStream$ to the history file.
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\item {\em Private} members

\begin{itemize}
\item Matrix$<$ double $>$ $Raw$: the raw dataset
\item Matrix$<$ double $>$ $TrainSetData$: the training set
\item Matrix$<$ double $>$ $TestSetData$: the test set
\item TwoSet $TrainTwoSet$: the {\em TwoSet} object for the training
      set
\item TwoSet $TestTwoSet$: the {\em TwoSet} object for the test set
\item vector$<$ double $>$ $minima$: a vector containing the minimum
      values of the columns in $Raw$
\item vector$<$ double $>$ $maxima$: a vector containing the maximum
      values of the columns in $Raw$
\item unsigned $nInput$: the number of input nodes
\item unsigned $nOutput$: the number of output nodes
\item double $threshold$: the threshold for discrete output
\item double $inUpperLimit$: the upper limit for a normalized input
      variate
\item double $inLowerLimit$: the lower limit for a normalized input
      variate
\item double $outUpperLimit$: the upper limit for a normalized output
      variate
\item double $outLowerLimit$: the lower limit for a normalized output
      variate
\item bool $discreteFlag$: a flag to indicate if the output is to be
      considered discrete (held to 0,1)
\item bool $rawLoadedFlag$: a flag to indicate if $Raw$ loaded
\item bool $trainLoadedFlag$: a flag to indicate if $TrainSetData$
      loaded
\item bool $testLoadedFlag$: a flag to indicate if $TestSetData$
      loaded
\item bool $trainTwoSetFlag$: a flag to indicate if the training set
      {\em TwoSet} object has been successfully constructed
\item bool $testTwoSetFlag$: a flag to indicate if the test set {\em
      TwoSet} object has been successfully constructed
\item bool $historyFlag$: a flag to indicate if operations will be
      logged to a history file, this file is {\em appended}
\item bool $minimaxFlag$: a flag to indicate if minima and maxima
      vectors have been set
\item string $historyFilename$: the name of history file
\end{itemize}

\item {\em Private} Utility functions

\begin{itemize}
\item void minimax( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $data$ ): computes the
      $minima$ and $maxima$ vectors from {\em Matrix} $data$
\item bool checkDiscrete( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $data$ ): returns
      true if the output columns of {\em Matrix} $data$ are discrete,
      false if otherwise
\item void normalize( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $data$ ): normalizes
      input variates of {\em Matrix} $data$, and if nondiscrete, its
      output variates {\em (note: $minima$ and $maxima$ vectors must
      have been previously set)}
\item void clear(): clears {\em DataSet Matrix} members and flags
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\section{TwoSet}

{\em TwoSet} is a specialized standalone object that is designed to
calculate outcome metrics such as sensitivity, specificity, predictive
value positive, predictive value negative and ROC area from a {\em
one} output {\em Model}. The {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix} contains 2
columns, one for the known outcomes and one for the {\em Model's}
outputs.  The {\em Model} output {\em must be} discrete.  The format
of this data {\em Matrix} is:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{cc}
$real_0$ & $test_0$ \\
$real_1$ & $test_1$ \\
$real_2$ & $test_2$ \\
$\ldots$ & $\ldots$ \\
$real_{n-1}$ & $real_{n-1}$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

where $real$ represents a known outcome, and $test$ is the {\em
Model's} output, for $n$ examples in the data {\em Matrix}, e.g.:

\begin{verbatim}
0.000000 0.002851
0.000000 0.002978
0.000000 0.002857
1.000000 0.571710
\end{verbatim}

For calculating ROC (receiver-operator characteristic) curve area, 2
methods are available.  One is the traditional method using trapezoids
and numerical calculation to calculate the area under the curve.  By
default, the number of trapezoids is set to the number of data points,
but that may be changed by the {\em setTrapThresholds()} method.
However, as should be evident, this method {\em underestimates} the
ROC area.  Wickens\footnote{Wickens, Thomas D., Elementary Signal
Detection Theory, Oxford University Press, New York, NY 2002,
pp. 60--74}\index{Wickens, Thomas D.} provides a statistical method to
calculate ROC area.  Here, \hyperref[invZarea]
{$z = Z(A) = \Phi^{-1}(A)$} is linearly modeled for sensitivity and
1 - specificity, and this linear model is used to calculate ROC area.
If enough data is available to bin and generate errors in the abscissa
and ordinate of this line, then a p-value will be generated to reflect
the probability of a least squares fit to that line, where a smaller
p-value is {\em worse} (closer to 1 is more linear).

The method $ROCarea( ostream\& )$ may be used to automatically choose
which method of calculating ROC curve are is used, depending on how
many data points are available.

Two errors may be thrown from this class.  The first is
$DivisionByZero$ where division by zero is encountered, generally in
calculating positive and negative predictive value.  The second is
$twoSetErr$, which will contain errors generated in statistical
calculation or linear modeling. $DivisionByZero$ always contains the
message ``division by zero'', whereas $twoSetErr$'s $what()$ member
will contain a message reflecting the type of error.  An example of
the
latter would be:

\begin{verbatim}
string reply;
cout << "What is the name of the file? ";
cin >> reply;
TwoSet data;
data.load( reply );
try {
   data.ROCarea( cout );
} catch ( TwoSet::twoSetErr& e ) { cout << e.what() << endl; }
\end{verbatim}

The {\em TwoSet} object consists of:

\begin{itemize}

\item {\em Public} methods

\begin{itemize}

The constructor TwoSet( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $M$ ) constructs and
loads {\em Matrix} $M$ into a {\em TwoSet} object

\item Methods for loading Matrices and files into a {\em TwoSet}
      object:
\begin{itemize}
\item void setMatrix( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $M$ ): Loads {\em Matrix}
      $M$ into a {\em TwoSet} object, returns nothing
\item void load( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as argument
      (string), and loads a file into the {\em TwoSet} data {\em
      Matrix}, returns nothing
\item bool loaded(): Returns a flag to indicate if the {\em TwoSet}
      data {\em Matrix} is loaded
\end{itemize}

\item {\em TwoSet} accessors:
\begin{itemize}
\item Matrix$<$ double $>$\& getData(): Returns the {\em TwoSet} data
      {\em Matrix}
\item bool save( string\& $filename$ ): Takes $filename$ as argument
      (string), and saves the {\em TwoSet} object into a file, returns
      flag indicating if save operation was successful
\item unsigned getNumElements(): Returns the number of elements in the
      {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item TwoSet\& insertReal( vector$<$ double $>$\& $v$ ): Insert vector
      $v$ into the real column of the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix},
      the number of elements of $v$ {\em must equal} the number of
      rows in the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item vector$<$ double $>$\& real( vector$<$ double $>$\& $v$ ):
      Populate vector $v$ with the real column of the {\em TwoSet}
      data {\em Matrix}, the number of elements of $v$ {\em must
      equal} the number of rows in the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item vector$<$ double $>$ real(): Get a new vector from the real
      column of the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item TwoSet\& insertTest( vector$<$ double $>$\& $v$ ): Insert vector
      $v$ into the test column of the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix},
      the number of elements of $v$ {\em must equal} the number of
      rows in the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item vector$<$ double $>$\& test( vector$<$ double $>$\& $v$ ):
      Populate vector $v$ with the test column of the {\em TwoSet}
      data {\em Matrix}, the number of elements of $v$ {\em must
      equal} the number of rows in the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item vector$<$ double $>$ test(): Get a new vector from the test
      column of the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item double real( unsigned $r$ ): An accessor to set or retrieve the
      element in row $r$ in the real column of the {\em TwoSet} data
      {\em Matrix}
\item double test( unsigned $r$ ): An accessor to set or retrieve the
      element in row $r$ in the test column of the {\em TwoSet} data
      {\em Matrix}
\end{itemize}

\item Accessors and methods for {\em TwoSet} calculated metrics
\begin{itemize}
\item void setThreshold( double $x$ ): Sets threshold value to $x$,
      any test element $>=x$ will be counted as $1$, whereas any test
      element $<x$ will be counted as $0$
\item double getClassAcc(): Returns classification accuracy
\item double getSens(): Returns sensitivity, throws exception
      TwoSet::DivisionByZero\& $e$, where $e$.what() is ``division by
      zero''
\item double getSpec(): Returns specificity, throws exception
      TwoSet::DivisionByZero\& $e$, where $e$.what() is ``division by
      zero''
\item double getPVP(): Returns predictive value positive, throws
      exception TwoSet::DivisionByZero\& $e$, where $e$.what() is
      ``division by zero''
\item double getPVN(): Returns predictive value negative, throws
      exception TwoSet::DivisionByZero\& $e$, where $e$.what() is
      ``division by zero''
\item void ClassTable( ostream\& $outputStream$ ): Outputs to
ostream\&
      the classification table
\end{itemize}

\item ROC area calculation by trapezoidal method
\begin{itemize}
\item void setTrapThresholds( unsigned $n$ ): Sets number of
      trapezoids to calculate ROC area to $n$
\item unsigned getTrapThresholds(): Returns number of trapezoids to
      calculate ROC area
\item double getTrapROCarea(): Returns ROC area calculated by
      trapezoidal method
\end{itemize}

\item ROC area calculation by statistical method
\begin{itemize}
\item void setNbins( unsigned n ): If data can be binned, sets the
      number of bins to $n$
\item unsigned getNbins(): Returns number of bins specified by user
\item void setBinSize( unsigned $n$ ): If data can be binned, sets
      number of elements to be contained in a bin to $n$
\item unsigned getBinSize(): Return bin size specified by user
\item void NbinThreshold( unsigned $n$ ): Sets the number of data
      points below which data will not be binned
\item unsigned getNBinThreshold(): Returns the number of data points
      below which data will not be binned
\item bool getBinned(): Returns $true$ if data was binned
\item void setNbinsSetsSize( bool $flag$ ): Set $flag$ if number of
      bins sets bin size
\item bool getNbinsSetsSize(): Returns $true$ if number of bins sets
      bin size
\item double getStatROCarea(): Returns statistical ROC area, throws
      exception TwoSet::twoSetErr\& $e$, where $e$.what() contains a
      statistical or linear regression error message
\item double getStatP(): Returns p-value for fitted line (smaller is
      worse)
\item double getStatChi2(): Returns chi-squared value for fitted line
\end{itemize}

\item Methods and accessors which output to ostream a ROC report which
      uses either or both the trapezoidal and/or statistical method
\begin{itemize}
\item void ROCarea( ostream\& $outputStream$ ): Outputs to ostream\&
      ROC area, and a report detailing method and results of
      calculation
\item void setROCReportFlag( $flag$ ): Set $flag$ true if both
      statistical and trapezoidal methods are to be reported, false to
      use either the trapezoidal method if number of data points $<$
      threshold ($calcThresh$) or statistical method if $>=$ threshold
\item bool getROCReportFlag(): Returns flag indicating if either
      (false) or both (true) of the statistical and trapezoidal
      methods
      are used and reported, according to the above description of
      setROCReportFlag($\ldots$)
\item void setROCthresh( unsigned $n$ ): Sets threshold for number of
      non-zero, non-one sensitivity and 1 - specificity data points
      under which statistics will not be used
\item unsigned getROCthresh(): Returns number of non-zero, non-one
      sensitivity and 1 - specificity data points under which
      statistics will not be used
\item void setROCSearchFlag( bool $flag$ ): Set $flag$ true if best
      $p$ and ROC areas are searched and reported within a specified
      range of number of bins
\item bool getROCSearchFlag(): Returns flag indicating if best $p$ and
      ROC areas are searched and reported within a specified range of
      bins
\item void setMinROCSearchBins( unsigned $n$ ): Sets minimum number of
      bins to search for best $p$ and ROC areas (must be $>2$)
\item unsigned getMinROCSearchBins(): Returns minimum number of bins
      to search for best $p$ and ROC areas
\item void setMaxROCSearchBins( const unsigned $n$ ): Sets maximum
      number of bins to search for best $p$ and ROC areas
\item unsigned getMaxROCSearchBins(): Returns maximum number of bins
      to search for best $p$ and ROC areas
\end{itemize}

\item Accessors for the Kolmogorov-Smirnov test\footnote{Adapted from
      W. H. Press et. al, Numerical Recipes in C, 2nd edition,
      Cambridge
      University Press, Cambridge, 1992, pp. 623--626}
      \index{Numerical Recipes in C}
      \index{Kolmogorov-Smirnov!Test}
\begin{itemize}
\item double getKSD(): Returns Kolmogorov-Smirnov statistic D
\item double getKSP(): Returns \hyperref[ks_p]{Kolmogorov-Smirnov $p$-
value}
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\item {\em Private} members

\begin{itemize}
\item Matrix$<$ double $>$ A: the {\em TwoSet} data {\em Matrix}
\item unsigned n: the number of elements in the {\em TwoSet} data {\em
      Matrix}
\item unsigned tp: the number of true positives
\item unsigned tn: the number of true negatives
\item unsigned fp: the number of false positives
\item unsigned fn: the number of false negatives
\item unsigned nThresholds: the number of thresholds for trapezoidal
      method
\item unsigned nBins: the number of bins for statistical ROC
\item unsigned binSize: the bin size for statistical ROC
\item unsigned binThresh: the number of data points under which data
      will not be binned
\item unsigned calcThresh: the number of data points under which
      statistics will not be used
\item double threshold: the threshold value
\item double statP: p-value for fitted line for statistical ROC
      calculation
\item double statChi2: chi-squared value for fitted line for
      statistical ROC calculation
\item double KSD: Kolmogorov-Smirnov test D value
\item double KSP: Kolmogorov-Smirnov test $p$-value
\item bool loadedFlag: a flag to indicate if the {\em TwoSet} data
      {\em Matrix} is loaded
\item bool thresholdFlag: a flag to indicate if the threshold is set
\item bool nBinFlag: flag indicates if number of bins determines bin
      size
\item bool binnedFlag: flag indicates if data was binned for
      statistical ROC calculation
\item bool statROCcalcFlag: flag indicates if statistical ROC area
      calculated
\item bool reportFlag: flag directs how to report statistical and/or
      trapezoidal ROC
\item bool searchFlag: flag indicates if largest p, largest ROC is
searched
\item bool KScalcFlag: flag indicates if Kolmogorov-Smirnov test
calculated
\end{itemize}

\item {\em Private} Utility functions
\begin{itemize}
\item void initialize(): Sets common initial values for constructors
\item void calculate(): Given a threshold, calculates tp, tn, fp, and
      fn
\item bool checkDiscrete( Matrix$<$ double $>$\& $M$ ): Returns true
      if the first column (representing real outcomes) of {\em Matrix}
      $M$ is discrete, false if otherwise
\item void statReport( ostream\& $outputStream$, unsigned $goodData$,
      unsigned $nBins$, double $AUC$, double $chi2$, double $p$ ):
      Utility method for ROCarea, outputs statistical report given
      ostream\& $outputStream$, number of non-zero, non-one data
      points $goodData$, number of bins $nBins$, area under the curve
      $AUC$, chi-square value $chi2$, and p-value $p$
\item void KScalc(): Utility method to calculate Kolmogorov-Smirnov
      test\footnote{Adapted from W. H. Press et. al, Numerical Recipes
      in C, 2nd edition, Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1992,
      pp.
      623--626} \index{Numerical Recipes in C} \index{Kolmogorov-
      Smirnov!Test}
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\section{What you really need to know about programming a Model}

\subsection{Object Type}\label{objType}

All concrete derived objects of {\em Model} inherit its protected
member, (string) $objType$, which is the name of the object.
Typically, the class constructor defines this member, for example:

\begin{verbatim}
class BareProp : public Network {
public:
   BareProp() { objType = "BareProp"; } // default constructor
\end{verbatim}

Abstract classes {\em do not} define $objType$. This member is
accessed by the {\em Model} method getType().

\subsection{Copy constructor and overloaded = operator}\label{copy_fx}

All derived objects of {\em Model} {\bf must} have a copy constructor
and overloaded $=$ operator.  Typically, both call a protected utility
function so that both copy the same members.  The protected utility
function typically begins with a call to the copy method of its
immediate base class so that it inherits all of the members of its
base class(es).  For example, suppose a hypothetical neural network
object named "LittleNet" is derived from {\em Network}, and contains
the following members:

\begin{verbatim}
unsigned nNodes; // number of nodes

Matrix< double > W; // weight Matrix

vector< double > I, // input vector
                 y; // output vector
\end{verbatim}

The following would be defined in the header file:

\begin{verbatim}
class LittleNet : public Network {
public:
   // Copy constructor
   LittleNet( const LittleNet& rhs );

   // Overloaded = operator
   LittleNet& operator= ( const LittleNet& rhs );

private: // or protected if the object is a base class
   // Copy utility
   void copy( const LittleNet& rhs );
\end{verbatim}

Which would then be coded in the $C++$ file as:

\begin{verbatim}
// Copy constructor
LittleNet::LittleNet( const LittleNet& rhs )
{
   LittleNet::copy( rhs ); // use the copy utility
}

// Overloaded = operator
LittleNet& LittleNet::operator= ( const LittleNet& rhs )
{
   if ( &rhs != this ) // check for self-assignment
      LittleNet::copy( rhs ); // use the copy utility

   return *this; // enables A = B = C
}

// Copy utility
void LittleNet::copy( const LittleNet& rhs )
{
   Network::copy( rhs ); // call immediate base object copy
   nNodes = rhs.nNodes;
   W = rhs.W;
   I = rhs.I;
   y = rhs.y;
}
\end{verbatim}

{\large \bf Remember:} {\bf if you add any member to any existing
class, you} {\bf \em must} {\bf insert a corresponding line of code to
copy it in the copy utility function of that class.}

\subsection{Implementation of pure virtual methods in {\em Model}}

Ultimately, all models map the inputs of each exemplar to the outputs.
Thus, most of the {\em Model} base class methods pertain directly to
this process.  If you are writing a Model, you need to implement the
following pure virtual methods:

\subsubsection{setDataSet}
The pure virtual method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void setDataSet( DataSet& $dataObj$ )
\end{verbatim}

loads a {\em DataSet} object into the derived {\em Model}.  The
protected member inherited from the base class {\em Model} is named
$theData$, so the first line of

\begin{verbatim}
void YourModel::setDataSet( DataSet& dataObj )
\end{verbatim}

where ``YourModel'' is the name of your derived {\em Model} object,
should be

\begin{verbatim}
theData = dataObj; // set "theData" to incoming DataSet
\end{verbatim}

The remainder of YourModel::setDataSet would then contain code that
tailors your model to the incoming {\em DataSet} object.  See
SimpleProp::setDataSet and BareProp::setDataSet for examples.

\subsubsection{train}
The pure virtual method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual double train()
\end{verbatim}

should train a {\em Model}, and return the final error (double) for
that model.  In the case of neural networks, training would involve
finding the weight vectors and Matrices that minimize final error. See
SimpleProp::train and BareProp::train for examples.

\subsubsection{reportAccuracy}
The pure virtual method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void reportAccuracy( ostream& outputStream )
\end{verbatim}

should report the accuracy of the trained model to an ostream
outputStream.  {\em If you are programming an object that extends}
Network, {\em e.g. a neural network, you} {\bf do not} {\em need to
implement this virtual method, as} Network {\em implements
reportAccuracy for you.}

\subsubsection{outputHeader}
The pure virtual method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void outputHeader( ostream& outputStream )
\end{verbatim}

should output a header to an ostream outputStream describing the
architecture of the derived {\em Model}.  See SimpleProp::outputHeader
and BareProp::outputHeader for examples.

\subsection{{\em Model} protected members}

The following are the protected members in {\em Model} that your
object needs to set or get:

\subsubsection{theData}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
DataSet theData
\end{verbatim}

represents the primary dataset, in the form of a DataSet object.  Your
setDataSet method needs to set this member.  See
SimpleProp::setDataSet and BareProp::setDataSet for examples.

\subsubsection{Train}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
Matrix< double > Train
\end{verbatim}

represents a Matrix that contains the training set input columns only,
typically constructed by a call within your setDataSet method to the
{\em Model} protected utility function extractInputMatrices().  See
SimpleProp::setDataSet and BareProp::setDataSet for examples.

\subsubsection{Test}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
Matrix< double > Test
\end{verbatim}

represents a Matrix that contains the test set input columns only,
typically constructed by a call within your setDataSet method to the
{\em Model} protected utility function extractInputMatrices().  See
SimpleProp::setDataSet and BareProp::setDataSet for examples.

\subsubsection{TrainOutput}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
Matrix< double > TrainOutput
\end{verbatim}

represents a Matrix that contains the training set output columns
only, typically constructed by a call within your setDataSet method to
the {\em Model} protected utility function extractOutputMatrices().
See BackProp::setDataSet for an example.\index{Backpropagation}

\subsubsection{TestOutput}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
Matrix< double > TestOutput
\end{verbatim}

represents a Matrix that contains the test set output columns only,
typically constructed by a call within your setDataSet method to the
{\em Model} protected utility function extractOutputMatrices().  See
BackProp::setDataSet for an example.\index{Backpropagation}

\subsubsection{builtFlag}

Set the protected member
\begin{verbatim}
bool builtFlag
\end{verbatim}

to {\em true} when the Model has been formally constructed, and its
architecture set.  See SimpleProp::setHidden and BareProp::setHidden
for examples.

\subsubsection{historyFlag}

Use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
bool historyFlag
\end{verbatim}

to decide if operations will be logged to a history file using the
protected {\em Model} utility function addHistory.  See SimpleProp and
BareProp code for examples, as most methods use addHistory.

\subsubsection{errorType}

Use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
bool errorType
\end{verbatim}

to determine if the type of error will be least mean squared
($errorType = 0$) or cross-entropy ($errorType = 1$).
\index{Error function!Cross-entropy}
\index{Error function!Least squares}
Typically, this member is used as an argument in {\em errorFunction}
constructors (see \hyperref[errFunc]{section \ref{errFunc}}, and
SimpleProp::trainSet() and BareProp::trainSet() as examples.)

\subsection{{\em Model} protected utility functions}

The following are the protected utility functions in {\em Model} that
your object will want to call:

\subsubsection{extractInputMatrices}

Call the utility function

\begin{verbatim}
void extractInputMatrices()
\end{verbatim}

in your setDataSet method to extract the input columns of the training
set into the protected {\em Model} Matrix $Train$, and the input
columns of the test set into the protected {\em Model} Matrix $Test$.

\subsubsection{extractOutputMatrices}

Call the utility function

\begin{verbatim}
void extractOutputMatrices()
\end{verbatim}

in your setDataSet method to extract the output columns of the
training set into the protected {\em Model} Matrix $TrainOutput$, and
the output columns of the test set into the protected {\em Model}
Matrix $TestOutput$.

\subsubsection{addHistory}

Pass an ostringstream object to the utility function

\begin{verbatim}
bool addHistory( ostringstream $outputStream$ )
\end{verbatim}

for that object to be added to the history file.  This method should
be called for every operation.  See SimpleProp and BareProp code for
examples, as most methods use addHistory.

\subsubsection{copy}

See section \ref{copy_fx} for details.

\section{Model}

In the following specification, methods and members that pertain
directly to coding a concrete derived {\em Model} implementation are
shown in \beginred red \endcolor.

{\bf Model} is the base object. {\em Model} provides access to the
dataset, and contains the virtual methods common to all modeling
techniques.  {\em Model} consists of:

\begin{itemize}

\item {\em Public} methods

\begin{itemize}

\item Copy constructor and overloaded $=$ operator, see section
\ref{copy_fx} for discussion:
\begin{itemize}
\item Model\& operator= ( const Model\& rhs )
\item Model( const Model\& rhs )
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to accessing the dataset to model:
\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred virtual void setDataSet( DataSet\& $data$ ): A pure
      virtual method that loads the {\em DataSet} object $data$ into
      the {\em Model} \endcolor
\item DataSet\& getDataSet(): Returns a the {\em Model's} dataset in
      the form of a {\em DataSet} object
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to training the model:
\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred virtual double train(): A pure virtual method that
      trains a {\em Model}, and returns the final error (double) for
      that model \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual void reportAccuracy( ostream\& $outputStream$
      ): A pure virtual method that reports the accuracy of the
      trained model to an ostream $outputStream$ \endcolor
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to logging to files or reporting to user:
\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred virtual void outputHeader( ostream\& $outputStream$ ):
      A pure virtual method which outputs a header to an ostream
      $outputStream$ describing the architecture of the model, {\em
      Model} type dependant \endcolor
\item void setHistory( bool $flag$ ): Sets history logging, which {\em
      appends} all operations to the history file
\item bool getHistory(): Returns the current state of history logging
\item void setHistoryFilename( string\& $filename$ ): Sets the name of
      the history file to string $filename$
\item string getHistoryFilename(): Returns the name of the history
      file
\item void setLastop( bool $flag$ ): Sets last operation logging,
      which {\em overwrites} a file to report the building (training
      in the case of {\em Iterative}) of a specific model
\item bool getLastop(): Returns the current state of last operation
      logging
\item void setLastopFilename( string\& $filename$ ): Sets the name of
      the last operation logging file to $filename$
\item string getLastopFilename(): Returns the name of the last
      operation logging file
\end{itemize}

\item Methods related to setting the output error function
\begin{itemize}
\item void setLMSerror(): Sets the output error function to least mean
      squared, which is \index{Error function!Least squares}
\begin{equation}
E = \frac{1}{2} \sum_{i=1}^{m} (y_i - o_i)^2
\end{equation}
where $E$ is the error, $m$ the number of outputs, $y$ is the known
value and $o$ the Model's output.
\item void setXEerror(): Sets the output error function to be
      \hyperref[xentropyfunk]{cross-entropy},
      \index{Error function!Cross-entropy} which is
\begin{equation}
E = \sum_{i=1}^{m} -y\log(o)-(1-y)\log(1-o)
\end{equation}
{\em Note: for the cross-entropy error function, the output } {\bf
must} {\em be } {\bf discrete}.
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\item {\em Protected} members

\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred DataSet $theData$: the primary dataset, in the form of
      a {\em DataSet} object \endcolor
\item \beginred Matrix$<$ double $>$ $Train$: a Matrix that contains
      the training set input columns {\em only}, constructed by a call
      to the protected utility function {\em extractInputMatrices()}
      \endcolor
\item \beginred Matrix$<$ double $>$ $Test$: a Matrix that contains
      the test set input columns {\em only}, constructed by a call to
      the protected utility function {\em extractInputMatrices()}
      \endcolor
\item \beginred Matrix$<$ double $>$ $TrainOutput$: a Matrix that
      contains the training set output columns {\em only}, constructed
      by a call to the protected utility function {\em
      extractOutputMatrices()} \endcolor
\item \beginred Matrix$<$ double $>$ $TestOutput$: a Matrix that
      contains the test set output columns {\em only}, constructed by
      a call to the protected utility function {\em
      extractOutputMatrices()} \endcolor
\item \beginred bool $builtFlag$: a flag to indicate if the {\em
      Model} has been formally constructed \endcolor
\item \beginred bool $historyFlag$: a flag to indicate if operations
      will be logged to a history file, this file is {\em appended}
      \endcolor
\item bool $lastopFlag$: a flag to indicate if model building
      (training in the case of {\em Iterative}) will be logged to a
      file describing the last operation, this file is {\em
      overwritten}
\item \beginred bool $errorType$: flag indicates type of error
function,
       $0$ if least mean squared, $1$ if cross-entropy \endcolor
       \index{Error function!Cross-entropy}
       \index{Error function!Least squares}
\item \beginred bool $builtFlag$: a flag to indicate the type of error
      function, 0 if least mean squared, 1 if cross-entropy \endcolor
      \index{Error function!Cross-entropy}
      \index{Error function!Least squares}
\item \beginred string $objType$: the name of the object, see
      \hyperref[objType]{section \ref{objType}} \endcolor
\item string $historyFilename$: the name of history file
\item string $lastopFilename$: the name of the file to log the last
      operation
\item string $errorLabel$: a string containing the name of the output
      error function
\end{itemize}

\item {\em Protected} Utility functions

\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred void extractInputMatrices(): Utility function which is
      intended to be called by the derived {\em Model} in its {\em
      setDataSet} method, and places the input columns of the training
      set in Matrix $Train$, and the input columns of the test set in
      Matrix $Test$ \endcolor
\item \beginred void extractOutputMatrices(): Utility function which
      is intended to be called by the derived {\em Model} in its {\em
      setDataSet} method, and places the output columns of the
      training set in Matrix $TrainOutput$, and the output columns of
      the test set in Matrix $TestOutput$ \endcolor
\item \beginred bool addHistory( ostringstream\& $outputStream$ ):
      Utility function to append the ostringstream object
      $outputStream$
      to the history file. \endcolor
\item \beginred void copy( const Model\& rhs ): Copy utility function,
      see section \ref{copy_fx} for discussion. \endcolor
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\section{What you really need to know about programming an
Iterative Model}

{\em Iterative} inherits methods setDataSet, train, reportAccuracy and
outputHeader from {\em Model}.  If you are programming an {\em
Iterative Model}, there are 2 pure virtual methods in {\em Iterative}
that you must also implement:

\subsubsection{trainSet}

{\em Iterative} handles models that require multiple passes
(iterations) to train.  Objects derived from {\em Iterative} must thus
provide a method to adjust a model by a single pass through the
training set, and {\em Iterative} will call this method iteratively to
reach the final trained state.  Thus the method:

\begin{verbatim}
virtual double trainSet()
\end{verbatim}

changes the components of a {\em Model} (e.g. weight Matrices and
vectors in a neural network), and returns the set error.  See
SimpleProp::trainSet() and BareProp::trainSet() for examples.

\subsubsection{classAccuracy}

With each iteration, {\em Iterative} reports the set error and
classification accuracy for the training and/or test sets if the
outputs are discrete.  Thus, the pure virtual method:

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void classAccuracy( ostream& outputStream )
\end{verbatim}

must output to an ostream object the classification accuracy for the
training and/or test sets.  {\em If you are programming a} Network
{\em object, you} {\bf do not} {\em need to implement this virtual
method, as} Network {\em implements classAccuracy for you.}

\subsubsection{getGradMax}

Most iterative algorithms employ some form of gradient descent, and
the iterative class can report the maximum absolute gradient and use
it to determine cessation of training.  The pure virtual method:

\begin{verbatim}
virtual double getGradMax()
\end{verbatim}

must be coded to return the maximum absolute gradient of the derived
object. {\em If you are programming a} Network {\em object, you}
{\bf do not} {\em need to implement this virtual method, as} Network
{\em implements getGradMax for you.}  However, you must code
\hyperref[packutil]{Network::pack()} to create the $Network$ protected
member $stackG$ so that Network::getGradMax() returns the proper
value.

\subsubsection{runHeader}\label{runHeaderSection}

If you are adding parameters specific to an {\em Iterative} derived
object that should be output to screen or log files prior to an {\em
Iterative} run, you will need to implement a runHeader method in the
derived class:

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void runHeader( ostream& outputStream)
\end{verbatim}

which outputs to an ostream object the specific parameters to be
displayed.  {\em If you are programming a} Network {\em object, you
will need to add code to this method in} Network.

\hspace{10pt} \linebreak

As with any derivation of {\em Model}, a copy utility function, copy
constructor and overloaded $=$ operator must be implemented.  See
section \ref{copy_fx} for details.

\subsubsection{iteration}

You may access the iteration index in your derived object by the
protected member unsigned $iteration$.

\subsubsection{boundsErrorFlag}

If your object can generate a numerical bounds error (see
\hyperref[errFunc]{section \ref{errFunc}} for an example of a method
which generates a numerical bounds error), then use the protected
member bool $boundsErrorFlag$ to indicate if such an error has been
thrown.

\subsubsection{gradMaxFlag}

Use the protected member bool $gradMaxFlag$ to determine if a gradient
is to be constructed in your derived object.

\section{Iterative}

{\bf Iterative} extends {\em Model}.  Neural computational algorithms
are iterative, and thus use {\em Iterative} as a base.  {\em
Iterative} manages methods and parameters related to iterating through
a dataset.

In the following specification, methods and members that pertain
directly to coding a concrete derived {\em Iterative} implementation
are shown in \beginred red \endcolor.

\begin{itemize}

\item {\em Public} methods

\begin{itemize}

\item Copy constructor and overloaded $=$ operator, see section
\ref{copy_fx} for discussion:
\begin{itemize}
\item Iterative\& operator= ( const Iterative\& rhs )
\item Iterative( const Iterative\& rhs )
\end{itemize}

\item Methods pertaining to stopping training
\begin{itemize}
\item void setMaxIterations( unsigned $maxIterations$ ): Sets the
      maximum number of iterations through the training set to
      $maxIterations$
\item unsigned getMaxIterations(): Returns the maximum number of
      iterations through the training set
\item void setMinStop( bool $minStopFlag$ ): Flag sets if a minimum
      error threshold will be used to stop training
\item bool getMinStop(): Returns flag indicating if a minimum error
      threshold will be used to stop training
\item void setMinError( double $minError$ ): Sets minimum error
      threshold value to $minError$
\item double getMinError(): Returns minimum error threshold value
\item void setChangeStop( bool $changeStopFlag$ ): Flag sets if
      training stops after error increases over 1 iteration
\item bool getChangeStop(): Returns flag indicating if change over 1
      iteration will be used to stop training
\item void setChange( double $\Delta$ ): Sets limit of change value to
      $\Delta$
\item double getChange(): Returns limit of change value
\item void setWindowStop( bool $windowStopFlag$ ): Flag sets if
      training stops if set error increases over a specified window
      width
\item bool getWindowStop(): Returns flag indicating if set error
      increases over a window width will be used to stop training
\item void setWindow( unsigned $window$ ): Sets the value of the
      window width to $window$
\item unsigned getWindow(): Returns specified window width value

\item void setGradStop( bool $gradMaxFlag$ ): Flag sets if training
      will stop when the maximum absolute gradient decreases below the
      limit
\item bool getGradStop(): Returns flag indicating if training will
      stop when the maximum absolute gradient decreases below the
      limit
\item void setGradMaxLimit( double $limit$ ): Sets the value of the
      maximum absolute gradient limit to $limit$
\item double getGradMaxLimit(): Returns the value of the maximum
      absolute gradient limit

\end{itemize}

\item Methods pertaining to printing while training
\begin{itemize}
\item void setLogPrint( bool $logPrintFlag$ ): Sets if prints out by
      powers of 10 ( 1-10 by ones, 10-100 by 10s, 100-1000 by 100s,
      etc. )
\item bool getLogPrint(): Returns flag indicating if printing by
      powers of 10
\item void setPrintCount( unsigned $printCount$ ): Sets the linear
      print counter to $printCount$
\item unsigned getPrintCount(): Returns the linear print counter
\end{itemize}

\item General methods
\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred virtual void setDataSet( DataSet\& $data$ ): A pure
      virtual method inherited from {\em Model} that loads the {\em
      DataSet} object $data$ into the {\em Model} \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual double train(): A pure virtual method
      inherited from {\em Model} that trains a {\em Model}, and
      returns the final error (double) for that model \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual void reportAccuracy( ostream\& $outputStream$
      ): A pure virtual method inherited from {\em Model} that reports
      the accuracy of the trained model to an ostream $outputStream$
      \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual void outputHeader( ostream\& $outputStream$ ):
      A pure virtual method inherited from {\em Model} which outputs a
      header to an ostream $outputStream$ describing the architecture
      of the model, {\em Model} type dependant \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual double trainSet(): A pure virtual method which
      trains one iteration through the training set, {\em Iterative
      Model} type dependant, and returns the set error \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual void classAccuracy( ostream\& $outputStream$
      ): A pure virtual method which outputs classification accuracies
      to an ostream $outputStream$ for an {\em Iterative} output table
      \endcolor
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\item {\em Protected} members

\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred unsigned $iteration$: the iteration index \endcolor
\item unsigned $maxIterations$: the maximum number of iterations
      through the training set
\item bool $logPrintFlag$: flag indicates if printing by powers of 10
\item unsigned $printCount$: the value for the linear print counter
\item bool $minStopFlag$: flag indicates if a minimum error threshold
      will be used to stop training
\item double $minError$: minimum error threshold value
\item bool $changeStopFlag$: flag indicates if change in error over 1
      iteration will be used to stop training
\item double $delta$: value of change over 1 iteration
\item bool $gradMaxFlag$: flag indicates if training will stop when
      maximum absolute gradient decreases below $gradMaxLimit$
\item double $gradMaxLimit$: value of the maximum absolute gradient
      limit
\item bool $windowStopFlag$: flag indicates if set error increases
      over a window width will be used to stop training
\item unsigned $window$: value of the window width
\item \beginred bool $boundsErrorFlag$: flag indicates if out of
      numerical bounds error encountered (e.g. $\log(0)$) \endcolor
\end{itemize}

\item {\em Protected} Utility functions

\begin{itemize}
\item \beginred void copy( const Iterative\& rhs ): Copy utility
      function, see section \ref{copy_fx} for discussion. \endcolor
\item virtual void runHeader( ostream\& $outputStream$ ): A pure
      virtual utility function that outputs parameters specific
      to derived objects preceeding an Iterative run
\item \beginred virtual double getGradMax(): A pure virtual utility
      function that returns the maximum absolute gradient value
      \endcolor
\end{itemize}

\end{itemize}

\section{What you really need to know about programming a Network
Model}

\subsection{Implementation of pure virtual methods in {\em Network}}
\label{NetworkMethodsSect}

{\em Network} inherits methods setDataSet, train, reportAccuracy and
outputHeader from {\em Model}, and trainSet from {\em Iterative}, so
you must code all of these methods.  (See the respective methods in
SimpleProp and BareProp as examples.)  If you are programming a {\em
Network Model}, there are 7 additional pure virtual methods in {\em
Network} that you must implement in the concrete derived object:

\subsubsection{df}

The method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual unsigned df()
\end{verbatim}

must be coded to return the number of degrees of freedom of your {\em
Network} object, which is equal to the number of nodal connections.
See SimpleProp::df and BareProp::df as examples.

\subsubsection{randomize}

The method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void randomize()
\end{verbatim}

must be coded to randomize the weights in your {\em Network} object.
See SimpleProp::randomize and BareProp::randomize as examples.

\subsubsection{load and save}

The methods

\begin{verbatim}
virtual bool load( string& filename )
virtual bool save( string& filename )
\end{verbatim}

must be coded to load and save your {\em Network} derived object from
and to a file named ``filename''.  Typically the method save would use
your coded method outputHeader to write the {\em Network} object
architecture to a file, then the Matrices and vectors representing the
{\em Network} model.  As expected, load should do the opposite.  See
SimpleProp::load, SimpleProp::save, BareProp::load and BareProp::save
as examples.

\subsubsection{innerTrainSet}\index{Automatic stepsize
selection}\label{autoStep}

The method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual double innerTrainSet()
\end{verbatim}

must be coded in a {\em Network} object to calculate the gradient and
error.  The reason that another method in addition to the pure virtual
method trainSet inherited from {\em Iterative} must be coded is to
support automatic stepsize selection.  The strategy proceeds as
follows: trainSet is intended to call innerTrainSet to determine if
the set error would increase, and set the learning rate (``step
size'') accordingly lower if it would.  Thus, innerTrainSet calculates
the gradient and error, but does not necessarily update the weights.
If automatic stepsize {\em is not} selected, trainSet simply calls
innerTrainSet to calculate the gradient and error, and updates the
weights.  If automatic stepsize {\em is} selected, trainSet calls
innerTrainSet to calculate the gradient and error, and reduces the
stepsize before updating the weights if the error is found to
increase. See SimpleProp::innerTrainSet, BareProp::innerTrainSet and
BackProp::innerTrainSet as examples of the way trainSet and
innerTrainSet are coded to work together.

Richard Golden has suggested the following automatic stepsize
selection algorithm, which he terms the ``Crummy Back-tracking Line
Search Algorithm'':

\begin{itemize}
\item Let DELTAERROR be a small non-negative number (e.g., DELTAERROR
= 1e-10 or 0)
\item Let MAXLOOPS = a small positive integer (e.g., MAXLOOPS = 2 or 3
or 4)
\item Let GAMMA be some positive number less than 1 (e.g., GAMMA = 0.5
or 0.8)
\item Let LoopCounter = 0
\item Set ``candidate'' learning rate equal to 1.0
\item While Exit Loop is FALSE
\begin{itemize}
\item LoopCounter = LoopCounter + 1
\item Compute change to weights using current candidate learning rate
\item Evaluate the new ERROR at the weights you obtained to get a
``candidate error change''
\item If (``candidate error change'' $<$ DELTAERROR) OR (LoopCounter
$>$ MAXLOOPS), then EXITLOOP is set to TRUE
\item Else set current candidate learning rate = GAMMA [candidate
learning rate]
\item End While Loop
\end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Don't be fooled by the name--this algorithm is far from ``crummy''.

{\bf Note:} This algorithm is only to be used with off-line or
batch/epoch learning.

\subsubsection{forward}\index{Forward propagation}

The method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void forward( Matrix< double >& M, const unsigned r )
\end{verbatim}

must be coded to take a Matrix containing only the inputs of a
training or test set as one argument (typically the $Train$ or $Test$
inherited protected members from {\em Model}), the position of the
exemplar in the training or test set as a second argument, and derive
the {\em Network's} output.  {\em This is not returned by the method.
This is very important: in single output} Networks, {\em the
inherited} Network {\em protected member o would be set by this
method, and in multiple output} Networks, {\em the inherited} Network
{\em protected member vector$<$ double $>$ output would be set by this
method.  Also very important, if sigmoidal output nodes are supported,
the inherited} Network {\em protected member x would be set by this
method, and in multiple output} Networks, {\em the inherited} Network
{\em protected member vector$<$ double $>$ xs would be set by this
method.}\index{Transfer function!Sigmoidal}

\subsubsection{removeInputs}\label{removeInputs}

The method

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void removeInputs( vector< unsigned >& v )
\end{verbatim}

must be coded to take as its argument a vector of unsigned
representing positions of input nodes, and to remove those nodes from
the {\em Network} derived object.  Typically, this would be
accomplished first by a call to
{\em DataSet}::removeInputs( vector$<$ unsigned $>$\& $v$ ),
followed by a call to {\em Model}::extractInputMatrices(), followed by
code which removes input nodes in the structures representing weights
in the {\em Network} object, typically {\em Matrix} columns.  See
SimpleProp::removeInputs and BareProp::removeInputs as examples.

\subsection{Code that must be added to {\em RegressNet}}

If you are creating a regressable {\em Network} object (and
chances are that you are,) you {\em must} add code to the methods
RegressNet::copy\_network() and RegressNet::network\_name().  See
section \ref{regresscode} for details.

\subsection{{\em Network} protected members}

The following are the protected members in {\em Network} that your
object needs to set or get:

\subsubsection{trainingType}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
unsigned trainingType
\end{verbatim}

determines the type of gradient descent algorithm of your neural
network. $trainingType = 0$ is defined as canonical backpropagation.
If you add other types of training to the {\em Network} class, you
must define a unique $trainingType$ for them.  Currently coded
algorithms include $trainingType =$

\begin{enumerate}
\item Conjugate gradient descent\index{Conjugate gradient descent}
\item Shanno algorithm\index{Shanno algorithm}
\end{enumerate}

{\bf NOTE:} Look closely at the code for Network::getGradMax(). You
will find that this code assumes that if $trainingType$ is not 0, then
$stackG$ will be constructed.  If this is not true for your
$trainingType$, then you must modify the if block in
Network::getGradMax().

\subsubsection{o}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
double o
\end{verbatim}

is the output for a single output network.  {\em If your} Network {\em
object supports single outputs, it must set this protected member,}
typically in the method forward.\index{Forward propagation}
Since the method trainSet will typically call the method forward,
trainSet will generally access the member $o$.  See
SimpleProp::forward and BareProp::forward for examples in setting the
protected member $o$, and SimpleProp::trainSet and BareProp::trainset
for examples in accessing the protected member $o$.

\subsubsection{x}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
double x
\end{verbatim}

contains the $x$ term for the sigmoidal output $o$ in a single output
network:\index{Transfer function!Sigmoidal}

\begin{displaymath}
o = 1/(1+e^{-x})
\end{displaymath}

{\em If your} Network {\em object supports single sigmoidal outputs,
it must set this protected member,} typically in the method forward.
\index{Forward propagation} See SimpleProp::forward and
BareProp::forward for examples in setting the protected member $x$.

\subsubsection{output}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
vector< double > output
\end{verbatim}

is the output for a multiple output network.  {\em If your} Network
{\em object supports multiple outputs, it must set this protected
member,} typically in the method forward.\index{Forward propagation}
Since the method trainSet will typically call the method forward,
trainSet will generally access the member $output$.

\subsubsection{xs}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
vector< double > xs
\end{verbatim}

contains the $\vec{\bf x}$ terms for the sigmoidal outputs
$\vec{\bf o}$ in a multiple output network:
\index{Transfer function!Sigmoidal}

\begin{displaymath}
\vec{\bf o} = 1/(1+e^{-\vec{\bf x}})
\end{displaymath}

{\em If your} Network {\em object supports multiple sigmoidal outputs,
it must set this protected member,} typically in the method forward.
\index{Forward propagation} See BackProp::forward for an example in
setting the protected member $xs$.\index{Backpropagation}

\subsubsection{randomLimit}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
double randomLimit
\end{verbatim}

is used to specify the range of randomized weights, which will be
between $- randomLimit$ and $+ randomLimit$.  Access this protected
member in your randomize method.  See SimpleProp::randomize and
BareProp::randomize for examples.

\subsubsection{weightsSetFlag}

Your derived {\em Network} object must set the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
bool weightsSetFlag
\end{verbatim}

to $true$ when the weights are set, either by randomization or loading
weights from a file.  See SimpleProp::randomize, BareProp::randomize,
SimpleProp::load and BareProp::load for examples of setting
$weightsSetFlag$ to $true$, and SimpleProp::setHidden and
BareProp::setHidden for examples of setting $weightsSetFlag$ to
$false$.

\subsubsection{biasFlag}

Use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
bool biasFlag
\end{verbatim}

to determine if your {\em Network} object has biases.

\subsubsection{eta}

Use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
double eta
\end{verbatim}

for the learning rate $\eta$ of your {\em Network} object.

\subsubsection{batchEpochFlag}\index{Backpropagation!Off-line}

Use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
bool batchEpochFlag
\end{verbatim}

to determine if your {\em Network} object uses batch/epoch learning or
not.  (See the description of classic off-line (``batch'') backprop
in \hyperref[learningAlgs]{section \ref{learningAlgs}}.)
\index{Backpropagation!Off-line}{\em Pay special attention to how the
learning rate is set.}  It is expected that the driver will guide the
user to the appropriate setting of the learning rate.  Note also that
``off- line'' learning is synonymous with `` batch'' or ``epoch''
learning, whereas ``on-line'' learning refers to updating the weights
with each exemplar.\index{Backpropagation!On-line}  Here, if
batchEpochFlag is {\em true}, ``offline'' learning is invoked, and if
batchEpochFlag is {\em false}, ``online'' learning is used.

\label{discussDecay}
\subsubsection{weightDecayFlag}\label{decayFlagSection}\index{Weight
decay}

Use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
bool weightDecayFlag
\end{verbatim}

to determine if your {\em Network} object uses weight decay.  (See the
discussion of \hyperref[deferrorfunk]{equation \ref{deferrorfunk} in
section \ref{objFunc}} for an explanation of weight decay.)  The
learning rate equivalent of equation \ref{deferrorfunk} is

% Note the necessity of \left. even though it doesn't show
\begin{eqnarray*}\index{Weight decay}
\vec w_{t+1} & = & \vec w_t - \eta \left. \D{E'}w \right|_{w_t} \\
& = & \vec w_t - \eta \left. \D{E}w \right|_{w_t} - 2\eta\lambda\vec
w_t \\
& = & (1-2\eta\lambda)\vec w_t - \eta \left. \D{E}w \right|_{w_t}
\end{eqnarray*}

where $(1-2\eta\lambda)$ is ``weight decay''.\index{Weight decay}
$\lambda$ may be set by either of 2 methods ($N$ is the sample number
size, see \hyperref[objFunc]{equation \ref{deferrorfunk} in section
\ref{objFunc}} for a discussion of $\sigma_w$):

\begin{itemize}

\item The complexity of the population objective function $>>$ sample
objective function:

\begin{equation}
\lambda = \frac 1{N2\sigma_w^2}
\end{equation}

\item The complexity of the population objective function $\simeq$
sample objective function:

\begin{equation}
\lambda = \frac 1{2\sigma_w^2}
\end{equation}

\end{itemize}

It is anticipated that the driver will guide the user to set the
appropriate value of $\lambda$, and {\em insure that the user knows
what he or she's doing if they change it!}

\subsubsection{decay}\index{Weight decay}

You can use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
double decay
\end{verbatim}

for the weight decay term $2\lambda$ of your {\em Network} object.
Note the multiple of 2 so that calculation of decay with the gradient
as in the right hand term of equation~\ref{gradcalc},
$(1/\sigma_w^2)[{\bf W} \;,\; {\bf b}]$, does not require the
additional step of multiplication by 2.

\subsubsection{regularizer}\index{Weight decay}

You can use the protected member

\begin{verbatim}
double regularizer
\end{verbatim}

if you need to access $\lambda$ directly, e.g. for of calculation of
your error function in equation \ref{deferrorfunk}, which like
$decayTerm$ only needs to be calculated once prior to iterative calls
to the TrainSet method, and is thus placed in the member function
runHeader, where the {\em Iterative} class will call it once prior to
iteratively calling the TrainSet method.

\subsubsection{decayTerm}\label{decayTermSect}\index{Weight decay}

In the protected member function runHeader in the {\em Network} class,

\begin{verbatim}
double decayTerm
\end{verbatim}

is defined to be $1-2\eta\lambda$ (or $1-\eta decay$), which only
needs to be calculated once prior to iterative calls to the TrainSet
method, and is thus placed in the member function runHeader, where the
{\em Iterative} class will call it once prior to iteratively calling
the TrainSet method.  You may therefore use $decayTerm$ as the weight
multiple for weight decay in your {\em Network} object if the decay is
not calculated with the gradient.  If the decay is calculated with the
gradient as in equation~\ref{gradcalc}, then $decay$ is used.  See
BareProp::TrainSet and SimpleProp::TrainSet for examples using
$decayTerm$.

\subsubsection{stackG}

The protected member

\begin{verbatim}
vector< double > stackG
\end{verbatim}

is a single vector which contains the entire weight gradient of your
{\em Network} derived object.  It is used by the protected utility
functions pack and unpack (\hyperref[packutil]{see below}).

\subsection{{\em Network} protected utility functions}

\subsubsection{Copy utility function, constructor and overloaded =
operator}

As with any derivation of {\em Model}, a copy utility function, copy
constructor and overloaded $=$ operator must be implemented.  See
section \ref{copy_fx} for details.

\subsubsection{runHeader utility function}

If you are adding parameters specific to a {\em Network} object that
should be output to screen or log files prior to an {\em Iterative}
run,
you will need to add code to the runHeader method in {\em Network}.
See \hyperref[runHeaderSection]{section \ref{runHeaderSection}}.

\subsubsection{pack and unpack}\label{packutil}

The methods

\begin{verbatim}
virtual void pack()
virtual void unpack()
\end{verbatim}

must be coded to convert the weight gradient structure of your {\em
Network} derived object to the member vector $stackG$ (pack), and vice
versa (unpack).  See SimpleProp::pack, SimpleProp::unpack,
BareProp::pack and BareProp::unpack as examples.

\subsection{Built-in gradient descent algorithms}

One of the really neat things about {\em Networks} that construct the
$stackG$ vector is that they may use the built-in advanced gradient
descent algorithms, Shanno's\index{Shanno algorithm} and conjugate
gradient descent\index{Conjugate gradient descent}.\footnote{Richard
M. Golden, Mathematical Methods for Neural Network Analysis and
Design, 1996, MIT Press, ISBN 0-262-07174-6, pp. 217--218, 221--222}
All the programmer needs to do is to construct the $stackG$ vector,
add the line of code
\begin{verbatim}
engine( trainingType, iteration );
\end{verbatim}
after the {\em Network} object gradient structure is calculated and
before the weights are updated, and these algorithms will
automagically be available for the user.  See SimpleProp::trainSet()
and BareProp::trainSet() for examples.

\subsubsection{Shanno algorithm}\index{Shanno algorithm}

Briefly, Shanno's algorithm proceeds as follows: let the computed
gradient be $\vec{g}$ ($stackG$), and the iteration be $t$ ({\bf
Note:} the iteration is {\em not} the exemplar index through the
training set. It is the actual iteration number for each calculated
gradient.  It should also be noted that this algorithm, as well as
conjugate gradient descent {\em is intended for off-line or
batch/epoch learning.}) Let the learning rate be $\gamma$.

\begin{itemize}
\item Step 0: Let $t=0$.
\item Step 1: Set $\vec{f}(t)=-\vec{g}_t$.  Go to step 3.
\item Step 2: If $t$ is a multiple of the degrees of freedom of the
      {\em Network}, then go to step 1.  Otherwise, compute
      $\vec{u}_t=\vec{g}_t-\vec{g}_{t-1}$ and the scalars $a_t$,
      $b_t$, $c_t$ such that
\begin{itemize}
\item $a_t=\frac{\vec{f}(t-1)^T\vec{g_t}}{\vec{f}(t-1)^T\vec{u}_t}$,
\item $b_t=\frac{\vec{u}^T\vec{g_t}}{\vec{f}(t-1)^T\vec{u}_t}$,
\item $c_t=\gamma+\frac{|\vec{u}_t|^2}{\vec{f}(t-1)^T\vec{u}_t}$,
\end{itemize}
and descent vector
\begin{displaymath}
\vec{f}(t)=-\vec{g}_t+a_t\vec{u}_t+(b_t-c_ta_t)\vec{f}(t-1)
\end{displaymath}
\item Step 3: Update the weights by
$\vec{w}(t+1)=\vec{w}(t)+\gamma\vec{f}(t)$.
Let $t=t+1$.  Go to step 2.
\end{itemize}

Interested readers are directed to Richard Golden's book, pp.
217--218. It should be noted that in the highly optimized code in
Network::shanno(unsigned $iteration$), $lastF$ refers to
$-\vec{f}(t-1)$.

\subsubsection{Conjugate gradient descent}\index{Conjugate gradient
descent}

Conjugate gradient descent is a special case of the Shanno algorithm,
and briefly proceeds as follows: let the computed gradient be
$\vec{g}$ ($stackG$), and the iteration be $t$ ({\bf Note:} again, the
iteration is {\em not} the exemplar index through the training set. It
is the actual iteration number for each calculated gradient.  It
should also be noted that this algorithm, as well as Shanno's, {\em is
intended for off-line or batch/epoch learning.}) Let the learning rate
be $\gamma$.

\begin{itemize}
\item Step 0: Let $t=0$.
\item Step 1: Set $\vec{f}(t)=-\vec{g}_t$.  Go to step 3.
\item Step 2: If $t$ is a multiple of the degrees of freedom of the
      {\em Network}, then go to step 1.  Otherwise, compute
      $\vec{u}_t=\vec{g}_t-\vec{g}_{t-1}$ and the scalar $b_t$
      such that
\begin{displaymath}
b_t=\frac{\vec{u}^T\vec{g_t}}{\vec{u}^T_t\vec{f}(t-1)}
\end{displaymath}
and descent vector
\begin{displaymath}
\vec{f}(t)=-\vec{g}_t+b_t\vec{f}(t-1)
\end{displaymath}
\item Step 3: Update the weights by
$\vec{w}(t+1)=\vec{w}(t)+\gamma\vec{f}(t)$.
Let $t=t+1$.  Go to step 2.
\end{itemize}

Interested readers are directed to Richard Golden's book, pp.
221--222. It should be noted that in the highly optimized code in
Network::CGD(unsigned $iteration$), $lastF$ refers to
$-\vec{f}(t-1)$.

\subsection{Calculation of the condition number}

The overloaded storeGrads($\ldots$) method, the reportCondNum(
ostream\&
$outputStream$) method and the $finalFlag$ flag may be used to compute
the condition number of a {\em Network} object.  The condition number
is the ratio of the largest to the smallest eigenvalue of the
symmetric matrix ${\bf B}$ computed by the outer product of the
average gradient vector and its transpose:

\begin{displaymath}
{\bf B} = {\bf g} {\cdot} {\bf g}^T
\end{displaymath}

The {\em Gnu Scientific Library (GSL)} eigenvalue routines were ported
for use in these methods.  Note that condition numbers of neural
networks are highly experimental, and in the current driver, the
condition number is calculated automatically only for logistic
regression (e.g. {\em Logistic}) type {\em Network} objects.  However,
implementation of condition number calculation is as general utility
methods in the {\em Network} class so that future programmers may
report
condition numbers of {\em Network} objects if desired.

Computation of the condition number for a {\em Network} derived object
proceeds as follows:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The overloaded storeGrads( unsigned $d$, unsigned $n$ ) method
is
      first used to initialize a gradient accumulator matrix ($grads$,
      which is transparent to the programmer and does not need to be
      included in added code) which will ultimately be used to compute
      the ${\bf B}$ matrix.  Arguments for this overloaded method are
      $d$, the dimension (size) of the gradient vector, and $n$, the
      number of exemplars in the dataset.
\item The gradient vector $stackG$ must be created using the $pack()$
      method.
\item Because methods involved in computation of the condition number
      are highly intensive, and because the condition number only
      requires
      the final average gradient vector, the $finalFlag$ flag is used
      to indicate a final iteration for the following step $\ldots$
\item The overloaded storeGrads( unsigned $n$ ) method is used to
      accumulate exemplar $n$'s gradient $stackG$ (which does {\em
      not} need to be passed as an argument, as it must be generated
      in code prior to use of this method.)  Note that the {\em
      Network} method reportCondNum(ostream\& $outputStream$) will
      automatically compute the average gradient, hence that
      particular exemplar's gradient is loaded (again, transparent to
      the programmer.)
\item reportCondNum(ostream\& $outputStream$) is used in methods which
      report results to the user, e.g. reportAccuracy( ostream\&
      $outputStream$ )
\end{enumerate}

See {\em Logistic} for example code, and see {\em SimpleProp} for
comments which indicate where code would be placed in a neural network
style implemented {\em Network} object.

\section{Network}

In the following specification, methods and members that pertain
directly to coding a concrete derived {\em Network} implementation are
shown in \beginred red \endcolor.

{\bf Network} extends {\em Iterative}. Algorithms with hidden layers,
weights, learning rates, transfer functions and other general
properties of neural networks are encapsulated in {\em Network}.

{\bf Note:} It is expected that in objects derived from {\em Network},
the method setDataSet inherited from {\em Model} be called {\em
before} the derived methods that specify object architecture, so that
the derived object may know the number of input nodes and size the
Matrices related to the dataset prior to completing its architecture.
This sequence of loading a dataset using the inherited {\em Model}
method setDataSet prior to establishing network architecture will be
invoked for all {\em Network} objects.

\begin{itemize}

\item {\em Public} methods

\begin{itemize}
\item Network\& operator= ( const Network\& rhs ): see section
      \ref{copy_fx} for discussion
\item Network( const Network\& rhs ): see section \ref{copy_fx} for
      discussion
\item \beginred virtual void setDataSet( DataSet\& $data$ ): A pure
      virtual method inherited from {\em Model} that loads the {\em
      DataSet} object $data$ into the {\em Model} \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual void outputHeader( ostream\& $outputStream$ ):
      A pure virtual method inherited from {\em Model} which outputs a
      header to an ostream $outputStream$ describing the architecture
      of the model, {\em Model} type dependant \endcolor
\item void setEta( double $\eta$ ): Sets the learning rate to $\eta$
\item double getEta(): Returns the learning rate $\eta$
\item void setBias( bool $flag$ ): Sets bias nodes in {\em Network}
\item bool getBias(): Gets boolean value indicating if bias nodes
      specified in {\em Network}
\item void setBatchEpoch( bool $flag$ ): Sets if batch/epoch learning
      is used in a {\em Network} object (See the description of
      classic off-line (``batch'') backprop in
      \hyperref[learningAlgs]{section \ref{learningAlgs}})
      \index{Backpropagation!Off-line}
\item bool getBatchEpoch(): returns if batch/epoch used
      \index{Backpropagation!Off-line}
\item void setWeightDecay( bool $flag$ ): Sets if weight decay is used
      in a {\em Network} object (See the discussion of
      \hyperref[discussDecay]{weight decay} in section
      \ref{decayFlagSection})\index{Weight decay}
\item bool getWeightDecay(): returns if weight decay used
      \index{Weight decay}
\item void setDecay( double $2\lambda$ ): Sets weight decay term to
      $2\lambda$ in a {\em Network} object\index{Weight decay}
\item double getDecay(): returns weight decay term $\lambda$
      \index{Weight decay}
\item void setTrainingType( unsigned $T$ ): Sets training type to $T$
\item unsigned getTrainingType(): returns training type
\item void setAutoStepSize( bool $flag$ ): Sets if automatic stepsize
      selection is used (See the discussion of
      \hyperref[autoStep]{automatic stepsize selection} in section
      \ref{autoStep})\index{Automatic stepsize selection}
\item bool getAutoStepSize(): returns if automatic stepsize selection
      is used
\item \beginred virtual unsigned df(): Returns the number of degrees
      of freedom, equal to the number of nodal connections \endcolor
\item void setRandomLimit( double $L$ ): Sets the limit for the random
      weights $-L$ to $+L$
\item \beginred virtual void randomize(): A pure virtual method which
      randomizes initial weights in a {\em Network} object \endcolor
\item getWeightsSet(): Returns a flag to indicate if weights set
\item \beginred virtual bool save( string\& $filename$ ): A pure
      virtual method which saves the {\em Network} object architecture
      and weights to a file named $filename$, returns true if
      successful \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual bool load( string\& $filename$ ): A pure
      virtual method which loads the {\em Network} object architecture
      and weights from a file named $filename$, returns true if
      successful \endcolor
\item \beginred virtual double trainSet(): A pure virtual method which
      trains one iteration through the training set, {\em $Network$}
