---------

Training time (HHHH:MM:SS) 0000:00:04=============================
Mon Jul 10 14:12:42  2000

\end{verbatim}

\subsection{black\_report}

This file has an identical format to error\_file, and is generated
when an input node or set of input nodes is held to 0, as in the
``black input'' feature.

\subsection{xentropy.full}\index{Error function!Cross-entropy}

This file contains the cross-entropy error calculated for a fully
trained network.  Like many features, it was designed early in
neUROn++'s life cycle, prior to combinatorial or stepwise regression
analysis.

\subsection{out-file}\label{outFile}

The file outfile contains one line with the number of examples in the
test set, followed by the number of output nodes, followed by the
threshold value.  Subsequent lines are pairs of the known value
followed by the network's guess.  For a one-output network, this takes
the form:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ccc}
{\em $N_x$ exemplars} & {\em $N_o$ outputs} & {\em threshold T} \\
\end{tabular}

\begin{tabular}{cc}
$known_1$ & $guess_1$ \\
$known_2$ & $guess_2$ \\
$known_3$ & $guess_3$ \\
$\ldots$ & $\ldots$ \\
$known_n$ & $guess_n$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

For example,

\begin{verbatim}
25 1 0.500000
1.000000 0.996711
1.000000 0.998407
1.000000 0.998407
1.000000 0.996711
1.000000 0.996711
1.000000 0.998407
1.000000 0.996711
\end{verbatim}
$\ldots$

\chapter{The Old Header File}

Prior to full C++ implementation in neUROn2++, as much of the network
description as possible was encoded in pre-processor definitions in a
header file, so that the compiled executable is as tailored as
possible to the data model.  The following section details these
pre-processor definitions.

\section{Global defines}

\subsection{Limits of computability}

The TOLERANCE preprocessor definition specifies the precision of the
compiler, e.g. 1e-8, 1e-12, etc.

\section{Neurocomputer architecture}

The following pre-processor definitions encode neurocomputer
architecture.

\subsection{BIAS}

Bias nodes are nodes held to 1 on a layer.  In neUROn++, BIAS ON
indicates bias nodes on all layers, whereas BIAS OFF indicates bias
nodes on no layers.

\begin{verbatim}
#define BIAS {1, 3, 4}
\end{verbatim}

would specify bias nodes on layers 1, 3 and 4.

\subsection{Layers}

In neUROn++, up to 3 hidden layers could be specified.  The following
pre-processor definitions describe layer architecture:

\begin{itemize}
\item NUMBER\_NODES\_MAX: specifies the maximum number of nodes on
      each layer, and is used for initializing arrays
\item NUMBER\_HDLAYER: specifies the number of hidden layers
\item NUMBER\_HIDDEN: specifies the number of hidden nodes in the 1st
      layer
\item NUMBER\_HIDDEN1: specifies the number of hidden nodes in the 2nd
      layer
\item NUMBER\_HIDDEN2: specifies the number of hidden nodes in the 3rd
      layer
\item NUMBER\_OUTPUT: specifies the number of output nodes
\item NUMBER\_INPUT: specifies the number of input nodes
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Activation function}

In neUROn++, the activation function is defined in the header file, as
is its derivative for the backpropagation algorithm.  The activation
function and its derivative are defined in macros:
\index{Backpropagation}

\begin{itemize}
\item TRANSFER\_FUNCTION(x)
\item DIFF\_TRANSFER(o)
\end{itemize}

Because backpropagation uses the sigmoidal activation function:
\index{Transfer function!Sigmoidal}

\begin{equation}
t_k = {1 \over {1 + e^{-net_k}}}
\end{equation}

and its derivative:

\begin{equation}
f\prime(net_k) = o_k(1-o_k)
\end{equation}

The following macro definitions are:

\begin{verbatim}
#define TRANSFER_FUNCTION(x) 1.0 / (1.0 + exp( -(x) ))
#define DIFF_TRANSFER(o) (o) * ( 1 - (o) )
\end{verbatim}

\section{Preparation}

The following pre-processor definitions describe data and model
processing prior to modeling.

\subsection{Data set}

The pre-processor definition TRAINING\_MAX specifies the maximum
number of exemplars in the training data set.

\subsection{Scaling}

\subsubsection{Input values}

As described in equation \ref{inputScale}, input values are scaled
according to the following formula:

\begin{displaymath}
x_{norm} = lbound + [({{x - min} \over {max - min}}) \times (hbound -
lbound)]
\end{displaymath}

Where $lbound$ is the lower boundary of the input value to be scaled,
$hbound$ is the upper boundary, $min$ is the minimum value of the
variable, $max$ is the maximum value of the variable, and $x$ is the
variable's value.  Thus, $lbound$ and $hbound$ for input variables are
specified in the header file as:

\begin{itemize}
\item INPUT\_LBOUND = $lbound$
\item INPUT\_HBOUND = $hbound$
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Output values}

In neUROn++, {\em nondiscrete} (e.g. the pre-processor definition
DISCRETE\_OUTPUT is set OFF) output values were scaled using:

\begin{itemize}
\item OUTPUT\_LBOUND
\item OUTPUT\_HBOUND
\end{itemize}

where OUTPUT\_LBOUND and OUTPUT\_HBOUND represented the low and high
bounds respectively of the output value.

\subsection{Randomizing initial weights}

Initial weights are randomized to a value bounded by $-bound$ to
$+bound$.  This $bound$ is specified by the pre-processor definition
RANDOM\_THRESHOLD, which is generally set to 0.5.  Thus, weights are
generally initialized to values between $-0.5$ and $+0.5$.

\section{Training control}

The following pre-processor definitions describe global control of
training, including report generation and how training ends.

\subsection{Reporting}

The following pre-processor definitions describe report generation:

\begin{itemize}
\item PRINT\_COUNT
\item WITH\_CLASS
\item OUTCOMES\_FILE
\item TEST\_SET\_STATS
\end{itemize}

PRINT\_COUNT specifies at which iteration error outcomes are reported.
It may take the form of a number, e.g. a PRINT\_COUNT of 1000 would
specify error reportage every 1000 iterations, or an equation, e.g.:

\begin{verbatim}
#define PRINT_COUNT (int) ( pow(10, (int) log10(set_count) ) )
\end{verbatim}

Specifies a highly useful exponential count.

WITH\_CLASS specifies if classification error in the training and test
sets are reported at PRINT\_COUNT.  These are useful to generate
graphs of neurocomputer training and identify such behavior as
overlearning.  Classification error is described as:

\begin{equation}
CE = {N_{correct} \over N_{total}} * 100\%
\end{equation}

Where $CE$ is the classification error (in either training or test
sets), $N_{correct}$ is the number of correct guesses, and $N_{total}$
is the total number of guesses.

If OUTCOMES\_FILE is specified ON, ``out-file'' is generated as
specified in section \ref{outFile}.

If TEST\_SET\_STATS is specified ON, specificity, positive predictive
value, and negative predictive value are reported in ``error\_file''
as specified in section \ref{errorFile}.

\subsection{Ending training}

The following pre-processor definitions control how training ends.

\begin{itemize}
\item ITERATIONS\_MAX specifies the maximum number of iterations
      at which training ends.  Even if the error is not at a minimum,
      training will end at the number of iterations specified by this
      pre-processor definition.

\item AUTO\_ERROR controls the next 3 definitions.  If AUTO\_ERROR is
      OFF, {\em all} of these definitions will be ignored.  If
      AUTO\_ERROR is ON, {\em any} any of these definitions is
      sufficient to end training.

\begin{itemize}
\item ERROR\_WINDOW\_WIDTH defines the number of iterations at which
      training will end if either the STOP\_INCREASE or the
      STOP\_AT\_MIN conditions are satisfied.

\item STOP\_INCREASE specifies that training will end if the error at
      any iteration $+$ ERROR\_WINDOW\_WIDTH is greater than the error
      at that iteration, e.g.:

	\begin{displaymath}
	E_{n+\Delta} > E_{n}
	\end{displaymath}
	where $n$ is the iteration, $E$ is the error, and $\Delta$ the
	ERROR\_WINDOW\_WIDTH.

\item If STOP\_AT\_MIN is ON, training will end if the change in error
      over the number of iterations specified by ERROR\_WINDOW\_WIDTH
      is less than MIN\_DELTA\_ERR, and

\item MIN\_DELTA\_ERR specifies the minimum change in error at which
      training will end over any ERROR\_WINDOW\_WIDTH.  Thus, training
      will end if

	\begin{displaymath}
	 E_{\Delta min} > E_{n+\Delta} - E_{n}
	\end{displaymath}

	where $n$ is the iteration, $E$ is the error, $\Delta$ is
	ERROR\_WINDOW\_WIDTH, and $E_{\Delta min}$ the
	MIN\_DELTA\_ERR.

\end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\section{Training}

The following pre-processor definitions specify how weights are
updated within a neural network, generally referred to as
``training''.

\subsection{Global definitions}

The following pre-processor definitions apply to any neural network
training methodology.

\subsubsection{Batch/Epoch}\index{Backpropagation!Off-line}

``Online'' learning refers to the scheme whereby weights are updated
with each training exemplar.\index{Backpropagation!On-line} Thus, if
$i$ refers to an individual training exemplar, then network weights
$w_i$ are updated by:

\begin{displaymath}
w_i \leftarrow w_i + \Delta w_i
\end{displaymath}

for each training exemplar, where $\Delta w_i
$ is the weight update value.

``Offline'' learning refers to the scheme whereby the weight updates
are calculated for the entire training set, summed, and applied to the
network's weights {\em after} the entire training set is processed:
\index{Backpropagation!Off-line}

\begin{displaymath}
w_i \leftarrow w_i + \sum \Delta w_i
\end{displaymath}

``Offline'' learning is often referred to as either ``Batch'' or ``
Epoch'' learning.\index{Backpropagation!Off-line}  If the
pre-processor variable BATCH\_EPOCH is ON, offline learning is
specified, if BATCH\_EPOCH is OFF, online learning is specified.
\index{Backpropagation!On-line}

\subsubsection{Discrete output}

If the pre-processor definition DISCRETE\_OUTPUT is ON, then output(s)
are defined to be 0 if the output value is less than a threshold
value, and 1 if the output value is greater than or equal to the
threshold value:

\begin{displaymath}
O \leftarrow O \geq T |^{1}_{0}
\end{displaymath}

where $O$ is the output value and $T$ is the threshold.  In neUROn++,
the threshold $T$ is specified in the raw training file as described
in section \ref{rawFile}.

\subsection{Backpropagation}\index{Backpropagation}

Backpropagation is specified by the pre-processor definition
CLASSIC\_BP.  Treatment of the backpropagation algorithm may be found
in a variety of texts such as Timothy Masters ``Practical Neural
Network Recipes in C++'' (Academic Press 525 B St, Suite 1900, San
Diego CA 92101-4495 www.apnet.com, www.academicpress.com ISBN
0-12-479040-2) 1993.

Briefly, canonical backpropagation proceeds as follows.  The input
vector is applied to the network, and outputs of the first hidden
layer are calculated by the activation function $t_k$ which is set to
be the the sigmoidal:\index{Transfer function!Sigmoidal}

\begin{equation}
t_k = {1 \over {1 + e^{-net_k}}}
\end{equation}

The outputs node values of the first hidden layer are applied to the
inputs nodes of the second hidden layer, etc.\ until the output
layer's
output node values are calculated.

The output node's weights are then updated by:

\begin{equation}
w_{jk} \leftarrow w_{jk} + \eta\delta_ko_j
\end{equation}

for learning rate $\eta$, $j$ output units and $k$ hidden units, where

\begin{equation}
\delta_k = (t_k - o_k)f\prime(net_k)
\end{equation}

$o_k$ is the known outcome value in the training set, and the the
derivative $f\prime(net_k)$ is:

\begin{equation}
f\prime(net_k) = o_k(1-o_k)
\end{equation}

Weights are then sequentially updated in the hidden layers in the
direction of {\em from} the ouput layer {\em to} the input layer,
hence the term ``backpropagation''.

\subsubsection{Learning rate}

The learning rate $\eta$ is specified by the preprocessor definition
ETA.

\subsubsection{Momentum}

Momentum adds a fraction of the previous iteration's weight update to
the current weight update:

\begin{equation}
\Delta w_t \leftarrow \Delta w_t + \alpha \Delta w_{t-1}
\end{equation}

where $\Delta w_t$ refers to the current weight update, and $\Delta
w_{t-1}$ to the previous weight update.  The ``momentum'' term is
$\alpha$.  The weight update rule in backpropagation then becomes:

\begin{equation}
\Delta w_t \leftarrow \eta\delta x + \alpha \Delta w_{t-1}
\end{equation}

The following pre-processor definitions specify momentum:
\begin{itemize}
\item MOMENTUM, if ON specifies that a momentum term will be applied.
\item ALPHA is the value of the momentum term.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Weight decay}\index{Weight decay}

Weight decay subtracts a fraction of the previous iteration's weight
(not weight update as in momentum) from the current weight update:

\begin{equation}
\Delta w_t \leftarrow \Delta w_t - d w_{t-1}
\end{equation}

where $\Delta w_t$ refers to the current weight update, and $w_{t-1}$
to the previous weight.  The ``decay'' term is $d$.  The weight update
rule in backpropagation then becomes:

\begin{equation}
\Delta w_t \leftarrow \eta\delta x - d w_{t-1}
\end{equation}

The following pre-processor definitions specify weight decay:
\begin{itemize}
\item WEIGHT\_DECAY, if ON specifies that a weight decay term will be
      applied.
\item DECAY is the value of the weight decay term.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Cross-entropy}\index{Error function!Cross-entropy}

Cross-entropy is an error function applied {\em only at the output
layer.}  It is defined by:

\begin{equation}
E = - t\log(o)+(1-t)\log(1-o)
\end{equation}

where $t$ is an output node's value derived by the network,
and $o$ is the known value of that output in the data set.

If the pre-processor definition XENTROPY is ON, then the cross-entropy
error function is calculated at the output node.  {\em It should be
clear that it is a requirement for XENTROPY that DISCRETE\_OUTPUT is
ON.}

\subsection{Shanno's algorithm}

This serves as an excellent example of the importance of
documentation.  A previous graduate student coded Shanno's memoryless
algorithm from Richard Golden's ``Mathematical Methods for Neural
Network Analysis and Design'' (1996) in neUROn++.  Experiments
demonstrated that training using this method resulted in speed
improvements up to 2 orders of magnitude.  Not only was the algorithm
itself coded, however, a very elegant method of automatically
switching between backpropagation and Shanno's algorithm was also
encoded.\index{Backpropagation}

Unfortunately, because the code was so poorly documented, subsequent
code modifications failed to include this module.  And also, as a
result, I am unable to reconstruct the meaning of the pre-processor
definitions here, only to list them as they appear in neUROn++ header
file.

\begin{verbatim}
#define SHANNO     OFF        // If SHANNO is ON, XENTROPY must be ON!

#define FAIL_LIMIT  2         // ensure shanno's algo working under
control
#define SWITCH_IT  60         // number of iterations for BP training
#define SHOW_SWITCH OFF       // ON to see the engine switch--hurt
performance!
#define SHANNO_MIN_DERROR 1e-4 // control of sensitivity of Shanno
switch

#define ALPHA_SHANNO 0.0001
#define BETA_SHANNO  0.999    // They are used for decide proper step
\end{verbatim}

\section{Feature extraction}

In neUROn++, feature extraction using Wilk's GLRT was originally
encoded in combinatorial fashion, and specified in the header file.
Later, with virtually no additional overhead, stepwise regression was
added as a set of internal modules.  Because these modules call the
training routines, the efficiency of the training routines do not
suffer by their presence in the compiled executable, only that the
executable is somewhat larger as a result.  The combinatorial feature
extraction definitions encoded in the header file were:

\begin{itemize}
\item If DEFAULT\_BLACK was ON, all combinations of features were
      automatically extracted.  For example, if features f1, f2 and f3
      represented the input variables, the following feature deficient
      networks were statistically compared to the full network (f1,
      f2, f3): (f1, f2), (f1, f3), (f2, f3), (f1), (f2), and (f3).
\item If INTERACT\_BLACK was ON, specific combination subsets of
      features could be feature extracted.  Using the above example,
      just (f1, f2), (f1, f3), (f2, f3) could be compared to the full
      network (f1, f2, f3).
\item MAX\_CLASS specified the maximum number of features to be
      extracted (not necessarily the number of input nodes, as more
      than one input nodes commonly encode a single feature.)
\item MAX\_CASE specified the maximum number of possible combinations
      of features, or $2^{MAX\_CLASS}$.
\item D\_NARY $= MAX\_CLASS + 1$.
\end{itemize}
\index{History!neUROn++|)}
